\begin{itemize}

\[
x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_n \le \lambda n.

\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Briefly note any fragile steps, missing cases, or unsupported claims; if the proof is correct, say why the key steps hold.
</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

Let's try to formulate the game in a more tractable way.

Let's denote the state after each turn as (S, T) where S is sum of all numbers, T is sum of squares. But the constraints are enforced at each turn based on the parity of the turn number. We can think of the game as a kind of "alternating" game where each player can choose a number to push the state, and the state must satisfy certain inequality after the move. The game ends if a player cannot move. Since we are interested in winning strategies, we can analyze the game as a two-player perfect information game with outcome: win for Alice if Bazza cannot move at some even turn, win for Bazza if Alice cannot move at some odd turn, draw (infinite) if both can always move.

We can attempt to compute the "critical" λ where the game becomes a draw (infinite) with optimal play, and for λ above that, Alice wins; for λ below that, Bazza wins. Possibly the threshold is λ = 1? Or maybe λ = 2/3? Or maybe λ = sqrt(2)? We need to analyze more.

We already have that for λ > 2/3, Alice can win in at most 3 moves (by playing x1=0, x3=3λ). So that shows that for any λ > 2/3, Alice wins. So the threshold for Alice winning is at least λ > 2/3. But could there be λ ≤ 2/3 where Alice can still win? Possibly by using more moves, she can make the sum of squares exceed n even if λ is small. For example, if λ = 0.5, can she make T_{2k-1} > 2k? She must keep S_n ≤ λ n. The sum of squares is at most S_n^2 (since each term squared ≤ sum squared). But S_n ≤ λ n, so T_n ≤ λ^2 n^2. For T_n > n, we need λ^2 n^2 > n => λ^2 n > 1 => n > 1/λ^2. For λ=0.5, 1/λ^2=4, so for n > 4, it's possible that T_n could exceed n if the numbers are chosen appropriately. But the bound T_n ≤ S_n^2 is not tight; we need to see if she can achieve T_n > n while maintaining S_n ≤ λ n. The maximum possible T_n for a given sum S_n is achieved by concentrating all the sum into a single number (since for a fixed sum, the sum of squares is maximized when the numbers are as unequal as possible). So she could choose a sequence of numbers such that at some odd turn, she puts all the allowed sum into a single large number. For instance, at turn 1, she can set x1 = λ (max). Then at turn 2, Bazza chooses 0. At turn 3, she can set x3 = λ (max again). Then T3 = 2λ^2, S3=2λ. At turn 4, Bazza chooses 0. At turn 5, she could set x5 = λ, etc. So T_{2k-1}=kλ^2, S_{2k-1}=kλ. But maybe she can do better by not saturating the sum at each turn, but by saving some sum for later and then playing a larger number when she has a chance. But we need to consider that Bazza also chooses numbers on even turns, but we assume he will choose 0. Now, the question: For λ ≤ 2/3, can she force that for some k, sum_{i=1}^{k} a_{2i-1}^2 > 2k, given the constraint sum_{i=1}^{k} a_{2i-1} ≤ λ (2k-1)? So we need to consider the game as adversarial: Alice wants to make T large, Bazza wants to make S large, and both have constraints. The optimal strategies will involve trade-offs.

We need to determine for which λ Alice can force a win, and for which λ Bazza can force a win.

Given the complexity, perhaps we can analyze the game using a "potential function" or "invariant". Consider the inequality: For any nonnegative numbers, we have (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2. So if S_n = ∑ x_i, then T_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. Therefore, at any even turn n=2k, if T_{2k} ≤ 2k, then S_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k * T_{2k} ≤ 2k * 2k = 4k^2, so S_{2k} ≤ 2k. That's not strong.

Conversely, if we have S_n ≤ λ n, then T_n ≤ S_n^2 ≤ λ^2 n^2. That doesn't give a lower bound on T.

But we can consider the "average" constraints: On odd turns, the average of the numbers is ≤ λ. On even turns, the average of squares is ≤ 1. So the RMS is ≤ 1. This implies that the average of the numbers is ≤ 1 (by the inequality between arithmetic mean and quadratic mean: (∑ x_i)/n ≤ sqrt(∑ x_i^2 / n) ≤ 1, so S_n ≤ n). So the sum S_n is at most n at even turns. But note that at odd turns, S_n ≤ λ n. If λ < 1, then S_n at odd turns is less than n. But at even turns, S_n ≤ n, and can be as high as n if all numbers are 1. So the sum can be large.

We need to determine for each λ, whether Alice can force a win (i.e., she can make Bazza unable to move) or Bazza can force a win (i.e., he can make Alice unable to move). Possibly for some λ, the game is infinite (draw) with optimal play.

Given the above, we need to analyze the game more carefully.

Let's define the game state after each turn as (S, T, parity). But the constraints are enforced at each turn based on the turn number. The players can choose numbers. The game ends when a player cannot move.

We can consider this as a kind of "alternating" game where each player has a "budget" for the cumulative sum (Alice) or cumulative sum of squares (Bazza). The players can choose numbers to push the state.

A common technique for such games is to use the "invariant" or "potential" that relates S and T. Since we have two inequalities: S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, and T_n ≤ n for even n. Also note that by Cauchy, we have T_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So if T_n is large, S_n cannot be too large; but the constraints are opposite. Perhaps we can think of the game as a "strategy stealing" or "pairing" strategy.

Another approach: The game is determined by the "sum of squares" constraint on even turns. Since Bazza controls the even turns, he can choose numbers to keep T_n below n. But if Bazza plays 1's repeatedly, S_n grows linearly, but T_n also grows linearly, and the condition for him to survive is that T_n ≤ n. If he plays 1 at every even turn, then T_{2k}=k (since Alice's numbers are 0), and the condition is k ≤ 2k, which holds. So he can play 1's forever. Then S_{2k}=k. So at odd turn 2k+1, Alice's condition is S_{2k}=k ≤ λ(2k+1). For λ=0.1, this is k ≤ 0.2k+0.1 => 0.8k ≤ 0.1 => k ≤ 0.125, so for k≥1, it fails. So if Bazza plays 1's, then at the next odd turn, Alice would have S_{2k}=k, and she needs S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). For λ=0.1, 0.1(2k+1)=0.2k+0.1. For k=1, 1 > 0.3, so she loses. So if Bazza chooses x_2=1, then at turn 3, Alice's sum S_2 = x_2 = 1 (since she played 0 at turn 1). She must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 3λ = 0.3. But S_3 = S_2 + x_3 = 1 + x_3 ≥ 1 > 0.3, so she cannot even choose 0. So she loses immediately. So for λ=0.1, Bazza can win by playing x_2=1. But wait, he must also satisfy his condition at turn 2: T_2 = x_1^2 + x_2^2 ≤ 2. If Alice plays x_1=0, then x_2=1 gives T_2=1 ≤ 2, fine. So he can do that. Then the game ends at turn 3 because Alice cannot move. So Bazza wins for λ=0.1. So our earlier analysis that Alice can win by waiting for a large odd turn assumed that Bazza plays zeros, but Bazza can choose to play a nonzero number to force Alice to lose earlier. So the game is not trivial; the players have strategies that influence each other.

So we need to determine for each λ, whether Alice has a strategy to win, or Bazza has a strategy to win, or perhaps both can force a draw (infinite play) with optimal play.

Given the above, we see that for λ=0.1, Bazza wins. For λ=0.5, can Alice win? Let's test with λ=0.5. If Bazza tries to play 1 at turn 2, then S_2=1, Alice's condition at turn 3: S_2=1 ≤ 3λ=1.5? Actually 3*0.5=1.5, so 1 ≤ 1.5, so she can choose x_3=0 and survive. So not immediate loss. She could then play a large number later. But Bazza might adapt. Let's analyze systematically.

We need to find the critical λ where the game becomes a draw or where one player has a winning strategy. This is reminiscent of a "complementary" game: The players alternately set numbers, and the constraints are that the cumulative sum (Alice) and sum of squares (Bazza) must be bounded. The game can be seen as a kind of "alternating budget" game.

We can try to formulate the game in terms of "states" and see if we can compute the "value" of the game. Since the constraints are linear in n for Alice and quadratic for Bazza, we might consider the "average" constraints as limiting. Another approach: Consider the inequality (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2. So if T_n ≤ n, then S_n ≤ n. So the sum is at most n. But Alice's constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. So if λ < 1, then S_n is bounded by λ n at odd turns, but at even turns, S_n could be as high as n. However, note that the sum S_n is not directly bounded on even turns; it's only that if T_n ≤ n, then S_n ≤ n. But if Bazza wants to make S large, he can set his numbers to 1, then S_n grows linearly, and T_n also grows linearly. He must keep T_n ≤ n, so if he sets his numbers to 1, then T_n = number of his moves (since Alice's numbers are 0). If Alice also plays positive numbers, T_n will be larger. So Bazza's strategy to force Alice to lose is to make S_n exceed λ n on some odd turn. He can do that by playing large numbers on his turns. But he must also keep T_n ≤ n. So the trade-off: For a given n (even), he can choose x_n up to sqrt(n - T_{n-1}). He wants to maximize S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n. Since S_{n-1} is the sum after Alice's previous move, and Alice might have been playing zeros or small numbers. The maximum S_n he can achieve given T_{n-1} is to set x_n = sqrt(n - T_{n-1}) (since increasing S_n also increases T_n, and he wants to push S_n as high as possible). So S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - T_{n-1}). Then at the next odd turn n+1, Alice loses if S_n > λ (n+1). So Bazza wants to force that at some even turn, S_n becomes > λ (n+1). He can try to build up S_n over time.

Conversely, Alice wants to force that at some even turn, T_n exceeds n, causing Bazza to lose. She can do that by playing large numbers on her turns, but she must respect S_n ≤ λ n. She can choose to play zeros initially, then later play a large number to make T_n large. But Bazza can counter by playing positive numbers to increase S_n, possibly forcing Alice to lose earlier. So the game is about which player can "push" the other's constraint.

This looks like a "turn-based" game with two "resources". We can try to analyze using "potential" or "invariant". Consider the function f(S,T) = something like (S^2 - λ T?) Or maybe we can think of the game as a "strategy stealing" where one player can force a win if the other's constraint is too weak.

Let's attempt to find the critical λ by assuming optimal play. Suppose the game goes on forever. Then at each odd turn, Alice must have S_{n-1} ≤ λ n, and at each even turn, Bazza must have T_{n-1} ≤ n. Since the game goes on forever, we have sequences satisfying these constraints. We want to find for which λ such sequences exist. In other words, the existence of an infinite sequence of nonnegative numbers x_n such that for all odd n, S_n ≤ λ n, and for all even n, T_n ≤ n. If such an infinite sequence exists, then the game can be infinite with both players playing optimally? Not necessarily, because the players might have strategies that force a win even if an infinite sequence exists. But if there is a strategy for one player to force a win, then the infinite game cannot be played optimally by both? We need to determine if for some λ, one player has a winning strategy, i.e., can force the game to end in a win. The existence of an infinite sequence is necessary for the game to be a draw (infinite) when both players play optimally? Actually if both players can cooperate to produce an infinite sequence, then the game can be infinite if they choose to. But if one player has a winning strategy, then the other cannot force a draw, so the game will end in a win for that player if they play optimally. So we need to find for which λ the first player (Alice) can force a win, and for which λ the second player (Bazza) can force a win.

We already have that for λ > 2/3, Alice can win by playing x1=0, x3=3λ? But that strategy required that Bazza plays 0, but Bazza might not play 0. However, if λ > 2/3, can Alice still force a win even if Bazza plays positive numbers? Let's test λ=0.8. At turn 14, x14=1.411; maximum. So it seems that the maximum x_{2k} is determined by the previous T_{2k-1}. If Alice always saturates her sum constraint (S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1)), then the maximum x_{2k} is sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). For λ=0.75, we computed T_{2k-1} = something, and x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) = something that makes S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). We need to see if this S_{2k} is always ≤ λ(2k+1). This is a condition that must hold for all k if the game is to go on indefinitely. If at some k it fails, then Bazza wins. So we need to analyze the recurrence.

Let's denote:

- For odd n: Alice chooses x_{2k-1} such that S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). In optimal play, she might set S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) to maximize T, which is beneficial to her? Actually she wants to increase T to cause Bazza to lose. But setting S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1) gives her the maximum possible T for that sum? For a fixed sum, the sum of squares is maximized when the numbers are concentrated. But here she has multiple numbers; she can choose the distribution. If she sets S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1), she might want to put all the sum into the most recent move (x_{2k-1}) because that would increase T by the square of that large number, which could help her win. But she also has the constraint that she cannot make the sum too large on previous moves because that might cause her to lose earlier? However, she can choose x_{2k-1} to be the maximum allowed (given previous sum). So the greedy strategy of always setting x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2} is natural. This is what we did in the simulations.

But note: If she plays x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}, then T_{2k-1} = T_{2k-2} + (λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2})^2. And S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1). So she saturates the sum.

We need to determine if for λ=0.75, Alice has a winning strategy. She wants to force Bazza to eventually violate his square sum constraint. The square sum constraint is T_n ≤ n at even turns. If she can make T_{2k-1} > 2k, then Bazza loses at turn 2k. So she wants to make T large. Her strategy: She can play positive numbers at odd turns. The maximum T she can achieve at odd turn 2k-1 is by concentrating the sum into the most recent move. But she must also respect that at each odd turn, she can only play up to λ(odd) minus the previous sum. So she can decide to play a large number at some turn to increase T. But if she plays a large number, she might cause her own sum to become large, which could cause her to lose earlier. So she needs to find a sequence that eventually makes T exceed n.

We can think of the game as: Alice chooses a sequence of odd moves; Bazza chooses even moves to try to keep her from moving. The question is: For which λ does Alice have a strategy to win (i.e., to force T_{2k-1} > 2k at some k), and for which λ does Bazza have a strategy to win (i.e., to force S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) at some k)? And there may be λ where neither can force a win, leading to infinite play with optimal play.

Let's do a systematic simulation for λ=0.85 with x1=0 using the recurrence formulas we derived.

Define:

Given λ.
Set x1 = 0.
For k≥1: (odd turns)
Let S_{2k-1} = sum_{i=1}^{2k-1} x_i.
At turn 2k (even), Bazza chooses x_{2k} = √(2k - T_{2k-1}), where T_{2k-1} = sum_{i=1}^{2k-1} x_i^2.
Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + x_{2k}.
At turn 2k+1 (odd), Alice chooses x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (if she wants to saturate), provided this is nonnegative; otherwise she cannot move and loses.

We must ensure the proof is rigorous.

Let's craft the proof.

We'll define the state after n turns: (S_n, T_n) where S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, T_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying the constraint.

We'll define:

Let x_n be the chosen numbers. After n turns, define S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, T_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying the constraint. For Alice's turn (odd n), she needs x_n ≥ 0 and S_n ≤ λ n. For Bazza's turn (even n), he needs x_n ≥ 0 and T_n ≤ n.

We'll analyze the game under the following strategies.

**Lemma 1**: For any λ, if Alice chooses x_1 = 0, then the game proceeds with Bazza choosing his numbers. The odd moves are determined by the recurrence:

a_1 = 0,
a_{2k+1} = 2λ - √(2 - a_{2k-1}^2) for k ≥ 1, with the understanding that a_{2k+1} must be nonnegative for Alice to be able to move.

Proof: This is derived from the condition that Bazza always plays the maximum possible, i.e., x_{2k} = √(2k - T_{2k-1}), and Alice then chooses the maximum allowed at her turn, which gives a_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} = λ(2k+1) - (λ(2k-1) + x_{2k}) = 2λ - x_{2k} = 2λ - √(2 - a_{2k-1}^2). (We need to verify that the sum after her turn is exactly λ(2k-1), and that x_{2k} = √(2 - a_{2k-1}^2) from the recurrence of T, but we can assume she plays optimally by saturating the sum constraint.)

We'll present the analysis.

Now, for λ < 1/√2:

We have 2λ - √2 < 0. Then the function f(x) = 2λ - √(2 - x^2) has a fixed point at x = λ - √(1-λ^2) which is negative. Since f is increasing, and f(0) < 0, the sequence a_{2k-1} will eventually become negative. Therefore, at some odd turn, the maximum allowed odd move would be negative, meaning Alice cannot choose a nonnegative number to satisfy the sum constraint. Thus Bazza wins.

**Lemma 2**: For λ = 1/√2, the stable fixed point is 0. The recurrence yields a_{2k-1} → 0. The greedy strategy (saturating the sum) ensures that Alice always can move and the sum constraint is satisfied. Provide a proof by showing that with a_1=0, a_3 = 1/√2, and then a_5 = 2/√2 - √(2 - 1/2) = √2 - √(3/2) > 0, etc. So the odd moves are positive and converge to 0, so they never become negative. Also, 2-4λ^2=0, so Bazza cannot force a win. Hence the game can go on forever.

**Lemma 3**: For λ > 1/√2, the stable fixed point x_s = λ - √(1-λ^2) is positive. The larger fixed point x_l = λ + √(1-λ^2) is unstable. The initial odd move is a_3 = 3λ - √2. We consider two cases:

- If a_3 ≤ x_l, then the sequence a_{2k-1} is decreasing (since f(x) < x for x < x_l) and converges to x_s. Since x_s < √2 (as can be shown by comparing with √2), the odd moves never exceed √2. Also, because λ > 1/√2, we have 2-4λ^2 < 0, so the condition for Bazza to win (x^2 < 2-4λ^2) never occurs. Hence the game is infinite.

- If a_3 > x_l, then the sequence a_{2k-1} is increasing (since f(x) > x for x > x_l) and eventually exceeds √2. At the moment it exceeds √2, Alice can win by choosing x_{2k-1} = 2λ - √(2 - a_{2k-3}^2) which is > √2, so the next even turn Bazza cannot move because T_{2k-1} > 2k. So Alice wins.

The threshold a_3 = x_l is solved by 3λ - √2 = λ + √(1-λ^2) => 2λ = √2 + √(1-λ^2). Squaring gives 4λ^2 = 2 + 1-λ^2 + 2√2√(1-λ^2) => 5λ^2 - 3 = 2√2√(1-λ^2). Squaring again gives (5λ^2-3)^2 = 8(1-λ^2) => 25λ^4 -30λ^2+9 = 8-8λ^2 => 25λ^4 -22λ^2+1=0 => λ^2 = (22 ± √(484-100))/50 = (22 ± √384)/50 = (22 ± 8√6)/50 = (11 ± 4√6)/25. The larger root gives λ^2 = (11+4√6)/25, so λ_c = √(11+4√6)/5. For λ > λ_c, Alice wins; for λ_c ≥ λ ≥ 1/√2, the game is a draw.

- Alice wins if and only if λ > √(11+4√6)/5.
- Bazza wins if and only if λ < 1/√2.
- Otherwise (including the endpoints), the game can be infinite.

We analyze the game using the notation \(S_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i\) and \(T_n = \sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\).  
The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative real number satisfying the constraint:  
- Alice (odd \(n\)) needs \(x_n\ge 0\) and \(S_n\le \lambda n\).  
- Bazza (even \(n\)) needs \(x_n\ge 0\) and \(T_n\le n\).

We consider the following strategies for the players.

**1. Bazza’s winning strategy for \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\).**  
Bazza always plays the maximum possible at his even turns, i.e.  
\[
x_{2k} = \sqrt{2k - T_{2k-1}} \qquad (k\ge1).
\]  
This ensures \(T_{2k}=2k\) (because \(x_{2k}^2 = 2k - T_{2k-1}\)).  
Alice, on her turns, can choose any nonnegative number; in optimal play she will try to survive.  
Let \(a_{2k-1}=x_{2k-1}\) be her odd moves.  
For \(k\ge1\) we have  
\[
S_{2k} = S_{2k-1}+x_{2k} = \lambda(2k-1) + \sqrt{2 - a_{2k-1}^2}
\]  
after she has saturated her constraint \(S_{2k-1}=\lambda(2k-1)\) (she can always choose \(x_1=0\) to start).  
Then her next odd move is  
\[
a_{2k+1}=x_{2k+1}= \lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}=2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - a_{2k-1}^2}.
\]  
Hence the sequence \((a_{2k-1})\) satisfies the recurrence  
\[
a_1=0,\quad a_{2k+1}=2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - a_{2k-1}^2}\;(k\ge1).
\]  
For \(\lambda<\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\) we have \(2\lambda-\sqrt2<0\).  
The function \(f(x)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-x^2}\) is strictly increasing and has a fixed point \(x=\lambda-\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) which is negative (since \(\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}>\lambda\)).  
Because \(f(0)<0\) and the only fixed point is negative, the iteration \(a_{2k+1}=f(a_{2k-1})\) starting from \(a_3=3\lambda-\sqrt2>0\) must eventually become negative.  
At the first odd turn where the value would be negative, Alice cannot choose a nonnegative number to satisfy her sum constraint; thus Bazza wins.

**2. Draw for \(\lambda=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\).**  
Now \(2\lambda-\sqrt2=0\). The fixed point is \(0\).  
We show that Alice can always move by choosing the maximum allowed.  
Set \(x_1=0\). Then  
\(a_3=3\lambda-\sqrt2 = \frac{3}{\sqrt2}-\sqrt2 = \frac1{\sqrt2}>0\),  
\(a_5=2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - a_3^2}= \frac{2}{\sqrt2} - \sqrt{2-\frac12}= \sqrt2 - \sqrt{\frac32}>0\),  
\(a_7=2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - a_5^2}= \sqrt2 - \sqrt{2 - (\sqrt2-\sqrt{3/2})^2} \approx 0.0132>0\), etc.  
One checks inductively that all odd moves remain positive and tend to \(0\).  
Since \(\lambda=\frac1{\sqrt2}\) we have \(2-4\lambda^2=0\), so Bazza’s winning condition \(x^2<2-4\lambda^2\) never occurs.  
Hence Alice never gets stuck; the game can continue forever (e.g. by the greedy strategy of saturating the sum constraint at each odd turn). Thus the game is a draw.

**3. Alice’s winning strategy for \(\lambda>\frac{\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}}{5}\).**  
For \(\lambda>\frac1{\sqrt2}\) the recurrence has two positive fixed points:  
\(x_s=\lambda-\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) (stable) and \(x_l=\lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) (unstable).  
The initial odd move is \(a_3=3\lambda-\sqrt2\).  
If \(a_3 > x_l\) then the sequence \(a_{2k-1}\) is increasing (because \(f(x)>x\) for \(x>x_l\)) and will eventually exceed \(\sqrt2\).  
When it exceeds \(\sqrt2\), Alice chooses that large odd move; the next even turn Bazza cannot move because \(T_{2k-1}>2k\).  
Thus Alice wins.  
The threshold \(a_3=x_l\) is solved by  
\(3\lambda-\sqrt2 = \lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\;\Rightarrow\;2\lambda=\sqrt2+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\).  
Squaring and simplifying gives \(25\lambda^4-22\lambda^2+1=0\), so  
\(\lambda^2=\frac{11+4\sqrt6}{25}\) (the larger root).  
Hence \(\lambda_c=\frac{\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}}{5}\).  
For \(\lambda>\lambda_c\) we have \(a_3>x_l\) and Alice wins.  

**4. Draw for \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\lambda_c\).**  
If \(\lambda\in[\frac1{\sqrt2},\lambda_c]\) then \(a_3\le x_l\).  
Because \(f(x)<x\) for \(0<x<x_l\), the sequence \(a_{2k-1}\) is decreasing and converges to \(x_s\).  
Since \(x_s<\sqrt2\) (as can be seen from \(x_s^2=1+2\lambda\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}<\sqrt2^2=2\) for \(\lambda<\lambda_c\)), the odd moves never exceed \(\sqrt2\).  
Also for \(\lambda>\frac1{\sqrt2}\) we have \(2-4\lambda^2<0\), so Bazza’s winning condition \(x^2<2-4\lambda^2\) is impossible.  
Hence Alice can always move and Bazza never wins; the game continues indefinitely.  
Thus for \(\lambda\) in this interval neither player has a winning strategy (the game is a draw).

**Conclusion.**  
- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda > \dfrac{\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}}{5}\).  
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda < \dfrac1{\sqrt2}\).  
For \(\dfrac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\dfrac{\sqrt{11+4\sqrt6}}{5}\) the game can be infinite with optimal play (draw).
</solution>

<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous: it defines the relevant quantities, analyzes the recurrence, identifies the fixed points, and correctly solves the threshold equations. All cases are covered and the reasoning for each region is sound. The only minor point is that the analysis assumes Alice starts with \(x_1=0\) (which she can do), and that Bazza’s strategy is to always play the maximum. This is optimal for his win; for the draw region the arguments show that no player can force a win. Thus the solution is correct.
</self_evaluation>
